<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity">Intensity</span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small noprint" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rain_in_Glenshaw,_PA.ogg" title="File:Heavy rain in Glenshaw, PA.ogg">Heavy rain in Glenshaw, Pennsylvania</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="336.8" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Heavy_rain_in_Glenshaw,_PA.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_7" preload="none" style="width:220px"><source data-bandwidth="34083" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (34 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Heavy_rain_in_Glenshaw%2C_PA.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="123760" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/cb/Heavy_rain_in_Glenshaw%2C_PA.ogg/Heavy_rain_in_Glenshaw%2C_PA.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">The sound of a heavy rain fall in suburban neighborhood</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr/><span class="selfreference"><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Rainfall intensity is classified according to the rate of precipitation, which depends on the considered time:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monjo1_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monjo1-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Light rain — when the precipitation rate is &lt; 2.5 mm (0.098 in) per hour</li>
<li>Moderate rain — when the precipitation rate is between 2.5 mm (0.098 in) - 7.6 mm (0.30 in) or 10 mm (0.39 in) per hour<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rainint_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-rainint-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UKint_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-UKint-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>Heavy rain — when the precipitation rate is &gt; 7.6 mm (0.30 in) per hour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rainint_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-rainint-106">[106]</a></sup> or between 10 mm (0.39 in) and 50 mm (2.0 in) per hour<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UKint_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-UKint-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>Violent rain — when the precipitation rate is &gt; 50 mm (2.0 in) per hour<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UKint_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-UKint-107">[107]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Euphemisms for a heavy or violent rain include gully washer, trash-mover and toad-strangler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
The intensity can also be expressed by rainfall erosivity <i>R-factor</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> or in terms of the rainfall time-structure <i>n-index</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monjo1_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monjo1-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_period">Return period</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/100-year_flood" title="100-year flood">100-year flood</a></div>
<p>The likelihood or probability of an event with a specified intensity and duration, is called the <a href="/wiki/Return_period" title="Return period">return period</a> or frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The intensity of a storm can be predicted for any return period and storm duration, from charts based on historic data for the location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The term <i>1 in 10 year storm</i> describes a rainfall event which is unusual and has a 50% chance of occurring in any 10-year period. The term <i>1 in 100 year storm</i> describes a rainfall event which is rare and which will occur with a 50% probability in any 100-year period. As with all probability events, it is possible, though improbable, to have multiple "1 in 100 Year Storms" in a single year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forecasting">Forecasting</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_precipitation_forecast" title="Quantitative precipitation forecast">Quantitative precipitation forecast</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rita5dayqpf.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="571" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Rita5dayqpf.png/200px-Rita5dayqpf.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Rita5dayqpf.png/300px-Rita5dayqpf.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Rita5dayqpf.png/400px-Rita5dayqpf.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rita5dayqpf.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of a five-day rainfall forecast from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrometeorological_Prediction_Center" title="Hydrometeorological Prediction Center">Hydrometeorological Prediction Center</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Quantitative Precipitation Forecast (abbreviated QPF) is the expected amount of liquid precipitation accumulated over a specified time period over a specified area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SERFC_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-SERFC-113">[113]</a></sup> A QPF will be specified when a measurable precipitation type reaching a minimum threshold is forecast for any hour during a QPF valid period. Precipitation forecasts tend to be bound by synoptic hours such as 0000, 0600, 1200 and 1800 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GMT" title="GMT">GMT</a>. Terrain is considered in QPFs by use of topography or based upon climatological precipitation patterns from observations with fine detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Starting in the mid to late 1990s, QPFs were used within hydrologic forecast models to simulate impact to rivers throughout the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Numerical_weather_prediction" title="Numerical weather prediction">Forecast models</a> show significant sensitivity to humidity levels within the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundary_layer" title="Planetary boundary layer">planetary boundary layer</a>, or in the lowest levels of the atmosphere, which decreases with height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> QPF can be generated on a quantitative, forecasting amounts, or a qualitative, forecasting the probability of a specific amount, basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Radar imagery forecasting techniques show higher <a href="/wiki/Forecast_skill" title="Forecast skill">skill</a> than model forecasts within 6 to 7 hours of the time of the radar image. The forecasts can be verified through use of rain gauge measurements, weather radar estimates, or a combination of both. Various skill scores can be determined to measure the value of the rainfall forecast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_agriculture">Effect on agriculture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heavy_Rains_in_Southern_Japan.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Heavy_Rains_in_Southern_Japan.gif/220px-Heavy_Rains_in_Southern_Japan.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Heavy_Rains_in_Southern_Japan.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heavy_Rains_in_Southern_Japan.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rainfall estimates for southern <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and the surrounding region from July 20–27, 2009.</div></div></div>
<p>Precipitation, especially rain, has a dramatic effect on <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. All <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> need at least some water to survive, therefore rain (being the most effective means of watering) is important to agriculture. While a regular rain pattern is usually vital to healthy <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, too much or too little rainfall can be harmful, even devastating to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crops" title="Crops">crops</a>. <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">Drought</a> can kill crops and increase erosion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> while overly wet weather can cause harmful <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a> growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Plants need varying amounts of rainfall to survive. For example, certain <a href="/wiki/Cactus" title="Cactus">cacti</a> require small amounts of water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> while tropical plants may need up to hundreds of inches of rain per year to survive.
</p><p>In areas with wet and dry seasons, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> nutrients diminish and erosion increases during the wet season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JS_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-JS-27">[27]</a></sup> Animals have adaptation and survival strategies for the wetter regime. The previous dry season leads to food shortages into the wet season, as the crops have yet to mature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Developing countries have noted that their populations show seasonal weight fluctuations due to food shortages seen before the first harvest, which occurs late in the wet season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Rain may be <a href="/wiki/Rainwater_harvesting" title="Rainwater harvesting">harvested</a> through the use of <a href="/wiki/Rainwater_tank" title="Rainwater tank">rainwater tanks</a>; treated to potable use or for non-potable use indoors or for irrigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Excessive rain during short periods of time can cause <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash floods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture_and_religion">In culture and religion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harar_Dance.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="307" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Harar_Dance.jpg/290px-Harar_Dance.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Harar_Dance.jpg/435px-Harar_Dance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Harar_Dance.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harar_Dance.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of a <a href="/wiki/Rainmaking_(ritual)" title="Rainmaking (ritual)">rain dance</a> being performed in <a href="/wiki/Harar" title="Harar">Harar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rain_deities" title="List of rain deities">List of rain deities</a></div>
<p>Cultural attitudes towards rain differ across the world. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climates</a>, people tend to be more stressed when the <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> is unstable or cloudy, with its impact greater on men than women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Rain can also bring joy, as some consider it to be soothing or enjoy the aesthetic appeal of it. In dry places, such as <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> or during periods of <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> rain lifts people's moods. In <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Setswana" title="Setswana">Setswana</a> word for rain, <i>pula</i>, is used as <a href="/wiki/Botswana_pula" title="Botswana pula">the name of the national currency</a>, in recognition of the economic importance of rain in its country, since it has a desert climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Several cultures have developed means of dealing with rain and have developed numerous protection devices such as <a href="/wiki/Umbrella" title="Umbrella">umbrellas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raincoat" title="Raincoat">raincoats</a>, and diversion devices such as <a href="/wiki/Rain_gutter" title="Rain gutter">gutters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Storm_drain" title="Storm drain">storm drains</a> that lead rains to sewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Many people find the scent during and immediately after rain pleasant or distinctive. The source of this scent is <a href="/wiki/Petrichor" title="Petrichor">petrichor</a>, an oil produced by plants, then absorbed by rocks and soil, and later released into the air during rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bear1964_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bear1964-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rain holds an important religious significance in many cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mersereau_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mersereau-132">[132]</a></sup> The ancient <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> believed that rain was the <a href="/wiki/Semen" title="Semen">semen</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sky_deity" title="Sky deity">sky-god</a> <a href="/wiki/Anu" title="Anu">An</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNajat1996_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNajat1996-133">[133]</a></sup> which fell from the heavens to inseminate his consort, the <a href="/wiki/Earth_goddess" title="Earth goddess">earth-goddess</a> <a href="/wiki/Ki_(goddess)" title="Ki (goddess)">Ki</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNajat1996_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNajat1996-133">[133]</a></sup> causing her to give birth to all the plants of the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNajat1996_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNajat1996-133">[133]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadians</a> believed that the clouds were the breasts of Anu's consort <a href="/wiki/Antu_(goddess)" title="Antu (goddess)">Antu</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNajat1996_133-3"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNajat1996-133">[133]</a></sup> and that rain was milk from her breasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNajat1996_133-4"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNajat1996-133">[133]</a></sup> According to Jewish tradition, in the first century BC, the Jewish miracle-worker <a href="/wiki/Honi_ha-M%27agel" title="Honi ha-M'agel">Honi ha-M'agel</a> ended a three-year drought in <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judaea</a> by drawing a circle in the sand and praying for rain, refusing to leave the circle until his prayer was granted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i>, the Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> preserves a prayer for rain made by the <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenians</a> to the Greek sky-god <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mersereau_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mersereau-132">[132]</a></sup> Various <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native American</a> tribes are known to have historically conducted <a href="/wiki/Rainmaking_(ritual)" title="Rainmaking (ritual)">rain dances</a> in effort to encourage rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mersereau_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mersereau-132">[132]</a></sup> Rainmaking rituals are also important in many African cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> In the present-day <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Governor_(United_States)" title="Governor (United States)">state governors</a> have held <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Prayer" title="Day of Prayer">Days of Prayer</a> for rain, including the <a href="/wiki/Days_of_Prayer_for_Rain_in_the_State_of_Texas" title="Days of Prayer for Rain in the State of Texas">Days of Prayer for Rain in the State of Texas</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mersereau_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mersereau-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_climatology">Global climatology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Earth_rainfall_climatology" title="Earth rainfall climatology">Earth rainfall climatology</a></div>
<p>Approximately 505,000 km<sup>3</sup> (121,000 cu mi) of water falls as precipitation each year across the globe with 398,000 km<sup>3</sup> (95,000 cu mi) of it over the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-chow-136">[136]</a></sup> Given the Earth's surface area, that means the globally averaged annual precipitation is 990 mm (39 in). Deserts are defined as areas with an average annual precipitation of less than 250 mm (10 in) per year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgsdesert_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgsdesert-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> or as areas where more water is lost by <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a> than falls as precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brittanica_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-brittanica-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deserts">Deserts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">Desert</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deserts.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="728" data-file-width="2184" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Deserts.png/300px-Deserts.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Deserts.png/450px-Deserts.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Deserts.png/600px-Deserts.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deserts.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Largest deserts</div></div></div><p>
The northern half of Africa is occupied by the world's most extensive hot, dry region, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a>. Some deserts are also occupying much of southern Africa : the <a href="/wiki/Namib" title="Namib">Namib</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalahari" title="Kalahari">Kalahari</a>. Across Asia, a large annual rainfall minimum, composed primarily of deserts, stretches from the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a> in Mongolia west-southwest through western Pakistan (<a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a>) and Iran into the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Desert" title="Arabian Desert">Arabian Desert</a> in Saudi Arabia. Most of Australia is semi-arid or desert,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> making it the world's driest inhabited <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a>. In South America, the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> mountain range blocks <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> moisture that arrives in that continent, resulting in a desertlike climate just downwind across western Argentina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andes_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Andes-48">[48]</a></sup> The drier areas of the United States are regions where the <a href="/wiki/Sonoran_Desert" title="Sonoran Desert">Sonoran Desert</a> overspreads the Desert Southwest, the Great Basin and central Wyoming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USatl_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-USatl-141">[141]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2471" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/220px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/330px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/440px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isolated towering vertical desert shower</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_desert">Polar desert</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">Polar desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polar_climate" title="Polar climate">Polar climate</a></div>
<p>Since rain only falls as liquid, in frozen temperatures, rain cannot fall. As a result, very cold climates see very little rainfall and are often known as <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">polar deserts</a>. A common biome in this area is the <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> which has a short summer thaw and a long frozen winter. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_caps" title="Ice caps">Ice caps</a> see no rain at all, making <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> the world's driest continent.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rainforests">Rainforests</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">Rainforest</a></div>
<p>Rainforests are areas of the world with very high rainfall. Both <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temperate_rainforest" title="Temperate rainforest">temperate</a> rainforests exist. Tropical rainforests occupy a large band of the planet mostly along the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. Most temperate rainforests are located on mountainous west coasts between 45 and 55 degrees latitude, but they are often found in other areas.
</p><p>Around 40–75% of all biotic life is found in rainforests. Rainforests are also responsible for 28% of the world's oxygen turnover.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monsoons">Monsoons</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">Monsoon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_trough" title="Monsoon trough">Monsoon trough</a></div>
<p>The equatorial region near the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a> (ITCZ), or monsoon trough, is the wettest portion of the world's continents. Annually, the rain belt within the tropics marches northward by August, then moves back southward into the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> by February and March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Within Asia, rainfall is favored across its southern portion from India east and northeast across the Philippines and southern China into Japan due to the monsoon advecting moisture primarily from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> into the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> The monsoon trough can reach as far north as the <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40th parallel</a> in East Asia during August before moving southward thereafter. Its poleward progression is accelerated by the onset of the summer monsoon which is characterized by the development of lower air pressure (a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_low" title="Thermal low">thermal low</a>) over the warmest part of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCFMRF_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCFMRF-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Similar, but weaker, monsoon circulations are present over <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> During the summer, the Southwest monsoon combined with <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_California" title="Gulf of California">Gulf of California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> moisture moving around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">subtropical ridge</a> in the Atlantic Ocean bring the promise of afternoon and evening thunderstorms to the southern tier of the United States as well as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHorel_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-JHorel-148">[148]</a></sup> The eastern half of the contiguous United States east of the <a href="/wiki/98th_meridian_west" title="98th meridian west">98th meridian</a>, the mountains of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">Sierra Nevada</a> range are the wetter portions of the nation, with average rainfall exceeding 760 mm (30 in) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-149">[149]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">Tropical cyclones</a> enhance precipitation across southern sections of the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Virgin_Islands" title="United States Virgin Islands">United States Virgin Islands</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mariana_Islands" title="Northern Mariana Islands">Northern Mariana Islands</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_the_Westerlies">Impact of the Westerlies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MeanMonthlyP.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MeanMonthlyP.gif/340px-MeanMonthlyP.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MeanMonthlyP.gif/510px-MeanMonthlyP.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MeanMonthlyP.gif/680px-MeanMonthlyP.gif 2x" width="340"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MeanMonthlyP.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-term mean precipitation by month</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">Westerlies</a></div>
<p>Westerly flow from the mild north Atlantic leads to wetness across western Europe, in particular <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, where the western coasts can receive between 1,000 mm (39 in), at sea-level and 2,500 mm (98 in), on the mountains of rain per year. <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a>, Norway is one of the more famous European rain-cities with its yearly precipitation of 2,250 mm (89 in) on average. During the fall, winter, and <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">spring</a>, Pacific storm systems bring most of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> and the western United States much of their precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHorel_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-JHorel-148">[148]</a></sup> Over the top of the ridge, the jet stream brings a summer precipitation maximum to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>. Large thunderstorm areas known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoscale_Convective_Complex" title="Mesoscale Convective Complex">mesoscale convective complexes</a> move through the Plains, Midwest, and Great Lakes during the warm season, contributing up to 10% of the annual precipitation to the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño-Southern Oscillation">El Niño-Southern Oscillation</a> affects the precipitation distribution, by altering rainfall patterns across the western United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Midwest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> the Southeast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> and throughout the tropics. There is also evidence that <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> is leading to increased precipitation to the eastern portions of North America, while droughts are becoming more frequent in the tropics and subtropics.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wettest_known_locations">Wettest known locations</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cherrapunji" title="Cherrapunji">Cherrapunji</a>, situated on the southern slopes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya" title="Himalaya">Eastern Himalaya</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shillong" title="Shillong">Shillong</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is the confirmed wettest place on Earth, with an average annual rainfall of 11,430 mm (450 in). The highest recorded rainfall in a single year was 22,987 mm (905.0 in) in 1861. The 38-year average at nearby <a href="/wiki/Mawsynram" title="Mawsynram">Mawsynram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meghalaya" title="Meghalaya">Meghalaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is 11,873 mm (467.4 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The wettest spot in Australia is <a href="/wiki/Mount_Bellenden_Ker" title="Mount Bellenden Ker">Mount Bellenden Ker</a> in the north-east of the country which records an average of 8,000 mm (310 in) per year, with over 12,200 mm (480.3 in) of rain recorded during 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Big_Bog,_Maui" title="Big Bog, Maui">Big Bog</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Maui" title="Maui">Maui</a> has the highest average annual rainfall in the Hawaiian Islands, at 10,300 mm (404 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Mount_Waialeale" title="Mount Waialeale">Mount Waiʻaleʻale</a> on the island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaua%CA%BBi" title="Kauaʻi">Kauaʻi</a> achieves similar torrential rains, while slightly lower than that of the Big Bog, at 9,500 mm (373 in)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> of rain per year over the last 32 years, with a record 17,340 mm (683 in) in 1982.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Searching the web one can find different amounts (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Its summit is considered one of the rainiest spots on earth, with a reported 350 days of rain per year. <br/>
</p><p><br/>
<a href="/wiki/Llor%C3%B3" title="Lloró">Lloró</a>, a town situated in <a href="/wiki/Choc%C3%B3_Department" title="Chocó Department">Chocó</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, is probably the place with the largest rainfall in the world, averaging 13,300 mm (523.6 in) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCDCxrain_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCDCxrain-162">[162]</a></sup> The Department of Chocó is extraordinarily humid. Tutunendaó, a small town situated in the same department, is one of the wettest estimated places on Earth, averaging 11,394 mm (448.6 in) per year; in 1974 the town received 26,303 mm (86 ft 3.6 in),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the largest annual rainfall measured in Colombia. Unlike Cherrapunji, which receives most of its rainfall between April and September, Tutunendaó receives rain almost uniformly distributed throughout the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quibd%C3%B3" title="Quibdó">Quibdó</a>, the capital of Chocó, receives the most rain in the world among cities with over 100,000 inhabitants: 9,000 mm (354 in) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCDCxrain_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCDCxrain-162">[162]</a></sup> Storms in Chocó can drop 500 mm (20 in) of rainfall in a day. This amount is more than what falls in many cities in a year's time.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_Earth">Outside Earth</span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_rain" title="Diamond rain">Rainfalls of diamonds</a> have been suggested to occur on the <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giant planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SC-20131009_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-SC-20131009-167">[167]</a></sup> as well as on the <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giant planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-20170825_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-20170825-168">[168]</a></sup> There is likely to be rain of various compositions in the upper atmospheres of the gas giants, as well as precipitation of liquid <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a> in the deep atmospheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo_ms_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-galileo_ms-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s largest natural satellite, infrequent <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> rain is thought to carve the moon's numerous surface channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary-Arizona_Icebox_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-Arizona_Icebox-171">[171]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">virga</a> evaporates 25 km (16 mi) from the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>  Extrasolar planet <a href="/wiki/OGLE-TR-56b" title="OGLE-TR-56b">OGLE-TR-56b</a> in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation)" title="Sagittarius (constellation)">Sagittarius</a> is hypothesized to have <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<ul><li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_aa"><a href="#ref_aa"><b><sup>a</sup></b></a></span> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_ab"><a href="#ref_ab"><b><sup>b</sup></b></a></span> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_ac"><a href="#ref_ac"><b><sup>c</sup></b></a></span>  The value given is the continent's highest, and <i>possibly</i> the world's, depending on measurement practices, procedures and period of record variations.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_bnone"><a href="#ref_bnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  The official greatest average annual precipitation for South America is 900 cm (354 in) at Quibdó, Colombia. The 1,330 cm (523.6 in) average at Lloró [23 km (14 mi) SE and at a higher elevation than Quibdó] is an estimated amount.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_cnone"><a href="#ref_cnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  Approximate elevation.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_dnone"><a href="#ref_dnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  Recognized as "The Wettest place on Earth" by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_World_Records" title="Guinness Book of World Records">Guinness Book of World Records</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wett_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-wett-174">[174]</a></sup></li></ul>
